    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_397192139" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/397192139" />
    <a title="David Schaafsma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma"><img alt="David Schaafsma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574997804p2/269235.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/397192139?book_show_action=true">Aug 21, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Schaafsma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Schaafsma" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma">David Schaafsma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=books-loved-2012">books-loved-2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=art">art</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=best-ya-ever">best-ya-ever</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=tweens">tweens</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=ya-summer-2015-class">ya-summer-2015-class</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=ya-romance">ya-romance</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer397192139" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1939114932896521904">I wrote this in August 2012: This is a very moving book by Calvin College English Professor Gary Schmidt that should be up for Printz and National Book awards. It's about the redemptive power of art in the face of trauma. It's also about the healing power of teaching, and about writing; all the characters are sharply etched, the dialogue is spot on, it's wrenchingly sad and also very funny, but is especially moving as Schmidt examines (through the teaching of an artist-librarian) the composition</span>
  <span id="freeText1939114932896521904" style="display:none">I wrote this in August 2012: This is a very moving book by Calvin College English Professor Gary Schmidt that should be up for Printz and National Book awards. It's about the redemptive power of art in the face of trauma. It's also about the healing power of teaching, and about writing; all the characters are sharply etched, the dialogue is spot on, it's wrenchingly sad and also very funny, but is especially moving as Schmidt examines (through the teaching of an artist-librarian) the composition of some Audubon bird drawings that serve as a model to reflect on both art and life. This stuff is especially moving, touching, at times thrilling. I have problems with a couple events I don't quite believe, but Schmidt is great after having convinced us of the power of artistic composition, that we see HIS composition, how all the parts fit, how they speak to each other. A central character here we really fall in love with, too. Great book, highly recommend.<br /><br />In June 2015 I reread it with a YAL summer class, so I'll say a bit more (but maybe don't read my review until you have read the book, I'd say); in a way it's more note-taking about what the book means for me:<br /><br />I forgave Schmidt the first time through for what I saw as a tiny"mistake," his having American liberal anti-war types spit on legless Chris as he returns home, saying they are happy he is disabled, having done the same to Viet Cong babies, etc. I thought that was a mistake and I still do. Or, I disagree with him, having lived through that period, and maybe I'm just more liberal, but I also just don't believe it. It's a small thing in the book. <br /><br />In the improbability category I list a drunken father so cruel he has "Mama's Boy" tattooed on his son's chest; myriad improbable human turnarounds (Ihow is it abusive Dad suddenly changes, and why, and is this consistent with what we know about him as a character and about abuse in general?), and just how is it Lil, a girl with no acting experience, gets a plumb part in a Broadway show where she plays a character from Jane Eyre much like her own story!, and how is it, when on opening night Lil goes ill, does Hero Doug get to play the part of this same girl and does it to thunderous applause? And also Joe Pepitone, Doug's 60s Yankees hero, shows up for his one performance??! Come on! How can I justify saying a book like this book is still awesome?!<br /><br />I'll answer that question, and take my sweet time, thank you. I'm willing to accept such apparent narrative silliness because these moments and moves happen in the context of Doug, our narrator, reading 19th century gothic melodrama Jane Eyre, with all of its elements of romance, comedy and tragedy. The centerpiece of the book is Doug's exploring with artist-librarian Mr. Powell principles of composition in drawing through analysis and imitation of Audobon's (19th century) Birds of America, drawings of birds that apply almost equally to his (Schmidt's/Doug's) own craft of writing a somewhat comically melodramatic children's novel. Such principles include a repetition of language and images: A precise balancing of structural elements that identify it as genre; elision (leaving things out so the reader/viewer can create her own images in the text), analogical thinking (vs. statistical analysis; both Audubon and Schmidt invite us to emotionally engage with their art, to read the world/Doug's life/our bodies/our lives through what we experience). <br /><br />The analysis of each drawing (which appear at the beginning of each chapter) is often like the drawings themselves breathtaking in their details and connections to life. Very moving, almost always. Sometimes downright breathtaking. I'm not lying, as Doug says. The art and Doug's engagement with it is the centerpiece of Doug's experience, his reclamation, his turn from jerk to good, and this is the book's centerpiece for us as well, the site of Schmidt's central teaching about reading art and literature. This writing has no silliness or improbability in it whatsoever. Jane Eyre, Birds of America and Okay for Now have this in common, that they are grounded in 19th-century romanticism and are great works that honor that tradition.<br /><br />So, Okay for Now operates in the context of 19th century melodrama. Doug reads emotional excess in Audubon, and I think it is there, but it is surely there in Jane Eyre, which Doug is reading in English class. The text he "writes," as first person narrator of this tale, imitates Bronte's tale in form and content and it draws on Audubon's dramatic drawings of birds. Schmidt, a medievalist in his scholarly life and a teacher still of British literature, teaches us to read such texts closely, caring about the forms in which they are written, but also teaches us to read with one's life in part through these texts. Direct addresses to the reader are a dimension of Bronte's text that deliberately invite us to imagine and live in that story, and Schmidt does the same with his story. "Dear reader, I kissed her. You know what that feels like?" Schmidt shows us that the best romantic tales invite us into the story. We are meant to be part of it, emotionally. <br /><br />Bronte's story is less comic than Okay For Now, of course. Okay, like Eyre, has plenty of painful and melodramatic and even tragic elements, but it is balanced in Okay by the comedic on almost every page. We balance painful realism with hope throughout. This is the kid book aspect of it, too, of course, but it helps see how a British lit scholar can also be a kid book author. Just when we think Schmidt is being too harsh, too graphically bitter in his depiction of Doug's life, we are treated to something wonderful, fabulous, some moment of goodness, his mother's magical smile, just spectacular. In the end of the book great and terrible things happen rapid-fire, as in any melodrama or comedy, but we accept these events, we celebrate them, we laugh, we cry, because that's what happens in such books that remind us of hope and beauty as both real even in the face of real tragedies. Yep, dear reader, I wept, and in a children's book, and several times, though less about Doug's sad life than his encounters with beauty, which as a reader and writer and teacher of literature I found beautiful and true. This book is about what the world is like because the world is inhabited by terrible things like war that takes your brother's legs, terrible people (like Doug's Dad, and the many high school kids that bully and beat Doug up), but it is also what we hope the world can become through love and attention to beauty.<br /><br />One narrative compositional balance: Throughout he observes that just when things are going pretty good, something bad starts to happen. The reverse is also true in this story, and this little roller coaster ride is finally structurally satisfying and beautiful. Doug's relationship with Lil is also a romance, like Jane Eyre, though also like Jane Eyre this romance has its ups and downs. Some romances are happy, some sad, though both directions can satisfy in their own ways. You think it's sappy? Well, you need to see this story just as any story in the context of principles of composition within a genre, including children's literature, and YA romance. This book teaches without being didactic, and what it teaches about genre, and historical context, is in the best sense of what it might mean to learn about the world and art and literature. It's as all good books and art are, about the importance of close and passionate reading and interpreting the world. Oh, and this is a book about Doug's becoming a reader of art and literature as we are becoming that as he teaches us to read.<br /><br />Doug, in making the (somewhat improbable) and "romantic" effort to restore the Audubon book to its original form, operates according to a principle of composition: the attempt to restore wholeness, which also applies to broken lives like Doug's. To seek beauty in whatever forms we might find it and to understand that this is an aesthetic and life goal is to to live with humor and hope. To choose to be good versus choosing to be a jerk is not a trivial pursuit. Reading and art are not trivial pursuits. Okay for Now teaches us to pay attention, as all great art and literature does. It teaches us to care about the ways we go about crafting our art and life in useful and meaningful ways. Finally, I think this is a classic children's/tween/YA/all ages book (like all the greats, like Wrinkle in Time, does an age category finally matter?) one of the very, very best works I have read. I'm not lying.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1939114932896521904" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_review_397192139'><a id="like_count_review_397192139" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/397192139?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">19 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F9165406-okay-for-now">Like</a></span>

<div id="review_172210205" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/172210205" />
    <a title="Lynn Pribus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2546121-lynn-pribus"><img alt="Lynn Pribus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1313073105p2/2546121.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/172210205?book_show_action=true">May 30, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lynn Pribus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lynn Pribus" href="/user/show/2546121-lynn-pribus">Lynn Pribus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer172210205" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10800565096394914632">I did enjoy this YA novel -- but somehow it felt just a bit "off." As if it was written for Newberry Award judges rather than kids, even if Doug often says "I'm not lying." <br/><br/>Kids don't know about Audubon without some explanation (which could easily have been provided by the Wise Older Man at the library.) Kids don't "get" references to "Dear Reader, I kissed her," even though Doug had been reading JANE EYRE in class -- a VERY unlikely eighth grade assignment, even in 1965. I think it was still SI</span>
  <span id="freeText10800565096394914632" style="display:none">I did enjoy this YA novel -- but somehow it felt just a bit "off." As if it was written for Newberry Award judges rather than kids, even if Doug often says "I'm not lying." <br /><br />Kids don't know about Audubon without some explanation (which could easily have been provided by the Wise Older Man at the library.) Kids don't "get" references to "Dear Reader, I kissed her," even though Doug had been reading JANE EYRE in class -- a VERY unlikely eighth grade assignment, even in 1965. I think it was still SILAS MARNER.<br /><br />Doug is a sweet kid with a father you love to hate. By the end of the book, the father has evidently completely reformed without any mention of him for many chapters and no explanation why he "saw the light."<br /><br />Doug's older (of two elder) brothers comes home from Viet Nam with serious problems which, of course, Doug is instrumental in solving.<br /><br />There are interesting adult characters along the way, but all in all, the style and structure of the book seemed flawed to me. Obviously, not true for the judges.<br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10800565096394914632" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_702123530" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/702123530" />
    <a title="Daniel (Attack of the Books!) Burton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/776624-daniel-attack-of-the-books-burton"><img alt="Daniel (Attack of the Books!) Burton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1501017725p2/776624.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/702123530?book_show_action=true">Aug 23, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Daniel (Attack of the Books!) Burton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Daniel (Attack of the Books!) Burton" href="/user/show/776624-daniel-attack-of-the-books-burton">Daniel (Attack of the Books!) Burton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/776624-daniel-attack-of-the-books-burton?shelf=young-adult-fiction">young-adult-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/776624-daniel-attack-of-the-books-burton?shelf=juvinile-fiction">juvinile-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/776624-daniel-attack-of-the-books-burton?shelf=adolescent-fiction">adolescent-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer702123530" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11485552751033039275">By the time I finished <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9165406.Okay_for_Now' title='Okay for Now by Gary D. Schmidt' rel='nofollow noopener'>Okay for Now</a>, I had read almost forty books this year. And while a large number of them were geared towards teens, especially boys. On my list this year there is <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/348878.James_Dashner' title='James Dashner' rel='nofollow noopener'>James Dashner</a>'s gripping tale<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6186357.The_Maze_Runner__Maze_Runner___1_' title='The Maze Runner (Maze Runner, #1) by James Dashner' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Maze Runner</a>, where <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7624.Lord_of_the_Flies' title='Lord of the Flies by William Golding' rel='nofollow noopener'>Lord of the Flies</a> meets Lost. In fantasy, I read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/25052.Brandon_Mull' title='Brandon Mull' rel='nofollow noopener'>Brandon Mull</a>'s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8306745.A_World_Without_Heroes__Beyonders___1_' title='A World Without Heroes (Beyonders, #1) by Brandon Mull' rel='nofollow noopener'>A World Without Heroes</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/38550.Brandon_Sanderson' title='Brandon Sanderson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Brandon Sanderson</a>'s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10137823.The_Rithmatist__Rithmatist__1_' title='The Rithmatist (Rithmatist #1) by Brandon Sanderson' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Rithmatist</a>, both excellent in their own right. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/249025.Robison_Wells' title='Robison Wells' rel='nofollow noopener'>Robison Wells</a>' <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10433900.Variant__Variant___1_' title='Variant (Variant, #1) by Robison Wells' rel='nofollow noopener'>Variant</a> kept me turning pages late into the night, as did <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/83308.David_Farland' title='David Farland' rel='nofollow noopener'>David Farland</a>'s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13323485.Nightingale' title='Nightingale by David Farland' rel='nofollow noopener'>Nightingale</a>.</span>
  <span id="freeText11485552751033039275" style="display:none">By the time I finished <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9165406.Okay_for_Now" title="Okay for Now by Gary D. Schmidt" rel="nofollow noopener">Okay for Now</a>, I had read almost forty books this year. And while a large number of them were geared towards teens, especially boys. On my list this year there is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/348878.James_Dashner" title="James Dashner" rel="nofollow noopener">James Dashner</a>'s gripping tale<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6186357.The_Maze_Runner__Maze_Runner___1_" title="The Maze Runner (Maze Runner, #1) by James Dashner" rel="nofollow noopener">The Maze Runner</a>, where <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7624.Lord_of_the_Flies" title="Lord of the Flies by William Golding" rel="nofollow noopener">Lord of the Flies</a> meets Lost. In fantasy, I read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/25052.Brandon_Mull" title="Brandon Mull" rel="nofollow noopener">Brandon Mull</a>'s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8306745.A_World_Without_Heroes__Beyonders___1_" title="A World Without Heroes (Beyonders, #1) by Brandon Mull" rel="nofollow noopener">A World Without Heroes</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/38550.Brandon_Sanderson" title="Brandon Sanderson" rel="nofollow noopener">Brandon Sanderson</a>'s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10137823.The_Rithmatist__Rithmatist__1_" title="The Rithmatist (Rithmatist #1) by Brandon Sanderson" rel="nofollow noopener">The Rithmatist</a>, both excellent in their own right. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/249025.Robison_Wells" title="Robison Wells" rel="nofollow noopener">Robison Wells</a>' <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10433900.Variant__Variant___1_" title="Variant (Variant, #1) by Robison Wells" rel="nofollow noopener">Variant</a> kept me turning pages late into the night, as did <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/83308.David_Farland" title="David Farland" rel="nofollow noopener">David Farland</a>'s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13323485.Nightingale" title="Nightingale by David Farland" rel="nofollow noopener">Nightingale</a>. <br /><br />Of all of them, though, none was more satisfying, fulfilling and memorable than <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6891947.Gary_Schmidt" title="Gary Schmidt" rel="nofollow noopener">Gary Schmidt</a>'s follow-up to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/556136.The_Wednesday_Wars" title="The Wednesday Wars by Gary D. Schmidt" rel="nofollow noopener">The Wednesday Wars</a>. Picking up right after the end of the adventures of Holling Hoodhood in The Wednesday Wars, Okay For Now takes the perspective of Doug Swieteck, Hoodhood's friend and little brother to Hoodhood's bully. When a job change takes them to "stupid Marysville," Doug finds himself an unlikely friend in the Hermione-like Lil Spicer, daughter of the local deli owner. Over the course of his eight-grade year, Doug will overcome prejudices, his own shortcomings, make new friends and mentors, and learn that his destiny is in his own hands.<br /><br />That all sounds so stereotypical and mundane, like what could be written on the back of almost any young adult novel. Believe me, then, when I say that there's nothing stereotypical or mundane about Doug's story. As he would say, "I'm not lying." Schmidt has a talent for making scenes equally humorous and tragic, and he cleverly and subtly uses language to show <i> and </i> tell who and what is on the up and up with Doug and what is not. <br /><br />"You know how that feels?" is a common phrase, something of a stage aside when Doug wants to accentuate his response to the situation, whether negative or positive. I found it clever that Doug would change then names of things subtly and without comment as their standing would change. For example, Christopher, Doug's brother and the bully from The Wednesday Wars begins the book as "my brother," but after an act of redemption becomes Christopher. Other labels that Doug uses with derision early in the book change, in connotation, as events unfold. In addition, Schmidt uses the imagery of art and Audubon's collection of bird paintings to bring out and describe Doug's experiences and growth. <br /><br />It's beautiful. <br /><br />Another reason I loved--yes, I loved it--Okay For Now is for its unique and deep demonstration of the bonds between males, the things that strengthen them, as well as the things that weaken them. Looking at both of the books, it's not hard to wonder if Schmidt has a soft spot in his heart for mothers and high standards for fathers, standards that he doesn't always think men meet. Though the novels are certainly full of traditional families with loving and honorable fathers--the book takes place in the late 1960s, so the traditional family is certainly still at the forefront in society--both the Hoodhood and Swieteck families are headed by less than satisfactory fathers at the outset, causing a major source of conflict for both Doug and Holling. <br /><br />Not only is his relationship with his father, and how his father's relationship with his mother, a major focus of the story, but so are the relationships between Doug and his brothers, including Christopher who I mentioned earlier, and Lucas who comes home from Vietnam. Also important to Doug's progress are relationships he develops with various other adults in the community, including teachers, librarians, and one eccentric playwright. <br /><br />Okay for Now is a beautiful story about a boy, and it's a story that will resonate with anyone, whether they remember what it was like to be 15 or not. With my own eight grade year now nearly two decades in the rear view mirror, reading Okay for Now took me back, reminding me of the growth and awkwardness of that tumultuous year and inspiring me to be more careful in my relationships.<br /><br />The year's not over yet, but Okay for Now will probably go down as the best book I will read this year, if not in the last several years. And I'm not lying. It's terrific, and I hope you will read it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11485552751033039275" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_194125436" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/194125436" />
    <a title="Walt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1005462-walt"><img alt="Walt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1235589111p2/1005462.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/194125436?book_show_action=true">Aug 03, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Walt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Walt" href="/user/show/1005462-walt">Walt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer194125436" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6014964446892427713">Terrific!<br/><br/>I'm not lying.<br/><br/>And I'm no chump in saying so.<br/><br/>Perhaps I should just leave it at that and hope you have the good sense to buy and read the book, if you haven't already.<br/><br/>Everyone should.<br/><br/>OKAY FOR NOW deserves the Newbery Award --- not a Newbery Honor, but the award, in my humble opinion.<br/><br/>And my opinion is humble, but it better be accurate in this case. I have to warn you, I have Christopher Swieteck waiting in the wings to do some arm-twisting.<br/><br/>This is a book about family --- mostly, the Swiete</span>
  <span id="freeText6014964446892427713" style="display:none">Terrific!<br /><br />I'm not lying.<br /><br />And I'm no chump in saying so.<br /><br />Perhaps I should just leave it at that and hope you have the good sense to buy and read the book, if you haven't already.<br /><br />Everyone should.<br /><br />OKAY FOR NOW deserves the Newbery Award --- not a Newbery Honor, but the award, in my humble opinion.<br /><br />And my opinion is humble, but it better be accurate in this case. I have to warn you, I have Christopher Swieteck waiting in the wings to do some arm-twisting.<br /><br />This is a book about family --- mostly, the Swieteck family --- and community --- mostly Marysville ---, and the institutions --- like the library and Audubon's collection in it, the local deli, the dream of Broadway, the military, the nearest professional sports team, the schools, the police department --- that flesh the people who occupy them out and make them better or, in some cases, worse.<br /><br />It is about the dynamics of art and sensitivity over against rationality's often heavy hand and stifling effect in ordinary life.<br /><br />It is about the way things of the heart affect things of the mind and vice versa; it is about people, good and bad, beautiful and ugly, sensitive and not.<br /><br />It is a love story about Doug and Lil and lost art and so so much more.<br /><br />It is well fleshed out, nicely sketched, colored almost to perfection. But not overdone.<br /><br />It has wings as light as feathers and it takes flight. And soars into the heavens.<br /><br />Hasn't almost everyone as a child or youth been cast into a new environment, whether in a move to a new town, going off to a new school, or finding oneself with different people in a new family configuration? Then there is the resulting disorientation, the inherent fear and anxiety. This is what happens to Doug Swieteck, Gary D. Schmidt's protagonist in his latest novel. How will Doug fare? How will be affected by the new people and institutions in his life? How will they? The narrative of the story provides the answers to these questions.<br /><br />Although, perhaps I shouldn't have read this book just when I did --- while my wife is suffering chronic cancer --- or the way I did --- reading it out loud, I'm so glad I did. But, I must say, it's very hard to read aloud in an emotional storm, seeing words blur on the page through a stream of fluid flowing from your eyes. <br /><br />I was born a few years before the protagonist of this adventure. I loved reminiscing, visiting the past's sights and sounds and events portrayed in the story, wallowing in it, reminiscing in my own private way. After all, I have my own Lil with whom I share a love for great art and literature. And life.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6014964446892427713" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review194125436">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2016861710?book_show_action=true">Jun 01, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rebecca McNutt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rebecca McNutt" href="/user/show/36977686-rebecca-mcnutt">Rebecca McNutt</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36977686-rebecca-mcnutt?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2016861710" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14985904349666912943">This incredible book deals with some very heavy themes, yet it never presents them in a sappy or preachy way, so in a sea of coming-of-age novels that come off like afterschool specials, <i>Okay for Now</i> dares to not sugarcoat anything but to still display the true meaning of family in dire times.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_197377480" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/197377480" />
    <a title="Jess" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/271355-jess"><img alt="Jess" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1363406354p2/271355.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/197377480?book_show_action=true">Aug 11, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jess" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jess" href="/user/show/271355-jess">Jess</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/271355-jess?shelf=juv">juv</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/271355-jess?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/271355-jess?shelf=national-book-award-finalist">national-book-award-finalist</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/271355-jess?shelf=odyssey-honor">odyssey-honor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/271355-jess?shelf=mock-newbery-2012">mock-newbery-2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer197377480" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8315919065017362149">Copied from my review of the audio version.<br/><br/>If you liked The Wednesday Wars, you must read this. Schmidt hits a lot of the same notes again, but his style - the voice, the characters, the whole thing - is so pitch perfect that I immediately wanted to start it over from the beginning (a rare feeling for me). In fact, I listened to the audio and then read the print version a few months later. The only downside to the audio is that you'll want to look up the Audubon illustrations in a book or online</span>
  <span id="freeText8315919065017362149" style="display:none">Copied from my review of the audio version.<br /><br />If you liked The Wednesday Wars, you must read this. Schmidt hits a lot of the same notes again, but his style - the voice, the characters, the whole thing - is so pitch perfect that I immediately wanted to start it over from the beginning (a rare feeling for me). In fact, I listened to the audio and then read the print version a few months later. The only downside to the audio is that you'll want to look up the Audubon illustrations in a book or online, because they're really key to the story. When I reread the print version, I found myself flipping back to that chapter's illustration anytime the image was discussed.<br /><br />The book's only flaw is that it stuffs in a few too many things plot-wise, but for me that never detracted from the story. Not perfect, but pretty close. And I'm not lying. <br /><br />Source: my public library<br /><br />A few notes I took on my second read (with the print version in hand), mostly prompted by discussions about the book in the Mock Newbery group. I wanted some specific examples of things I think Schmidt does really well in order to respond to points other readers made.<br /><br />P. 63: Doug ends one section with, "I stood there like a chump. You see how things never go right when you're feeling good?" The next section begins with his father saying, "You see how things never go right when you're feeling good?" To me, Doug and his brothers and his father are all part of a cycle of violence - watching the way Doug chooses to sometimes follow in his brothers' and father's footsteps, while other times he manages to break away. Here, he's obviously repeating a sentiment that's become ingrained after years of hearing his father talk this way.<br /><br />P. 84: Doug describes taking his mother out for ice cream and paying, and then says, "You know how that felt?" I love the way Schmidt does this with Doug's voice - the way Doug asks us a question that reveals so much more about his emotions than saying "that felt good." It's so in character for Doug.<br /><br />P. 143: The same thing happens again, only with a negative situation, when Doug is finally forced out of his gym shirt. "And what they saw--it's not any of your stupid business." It leaves you hanging and lets your imagination get to work, because you know it's bad. But without Doug ever coming out and saying it's bad.<br /><br />P. 156: The watches. I love Doug's pride in ownership here, and again he doesn't just say it, he shows how he feels, just like with Joe Pepitone's jacket.<br /><br />P. 171: When Doug goes to the paper mill and learns that the baseball and hundred dollars were sent home with his father, and rather than outing his father to Mr. Ballard, he just tells us, "It was just like my father said. You shouldn't count on anything." Just kills me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8315919065017362149" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1130797571" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1130797571" />
    <a title="Agnė" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22440207-agn"><img alt="Agnė" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1490978686p2/22440207.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1130797571?book_show_action=true">Dec 13, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Agnė" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Agnė" href="/user/show/22440207-agn">Agnė</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Skeptics of young adult literature</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Agnė by: </span>
      Self Respect Teen Lit Book Group
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22440207-agn?shelf=book-club-teen">book-club-teen</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22440207-agn?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22440207-agn?shelf=realistic-fiction">realistic-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22440207-agn?shelf=children">children</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22440207-agn?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22440207-agn?shelf=middle-grade">middle-grade</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22440207-agn?shelf=art">art</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1130797571" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16099461902513938754"><b>WHAT IT IS ABOUT:</b><br/><br/>“Okay for Now” by Gary Schmidt is a heartwarming young adult historical novel centered around eighth grader Douglas Swieteck. After Doug’s abusive father loses his job, the whole family moves to Marysville in upstate New York. In the new town, Doug’s older brother is accused of robbery, and Doug himself is struggling to be more than just a “skinny thug” that some townsfolk believe him to be. However, things start to look different when Doug befriends Lil Spicer, the daughter of </span>
  <span id="freeText16099461902513938754" style="display:none"><b>WHAT IT IS ABOUT:</b><br /><br />“Okay for Now” by Gary Schmidt is a heartwarming young adult historical novel centered around eighth grader Douglas Swieteck. After Doug’s abusive father loses his job, the whole family moves to Marysville in upstate New York. In the new town, Doug’s older brother is accused of robbery, and Doug himself is struggling to be more than just a “skinny thug” that some townsfolk believe him to be. However, things start to look different when Doug befriends Lil Spicer, the daughter of Spicer’s Deli’s owner, and discovers that the local library has a rare copy of John James Audubon’s book “Birds of America.”<br /><br /><b>THUMBS UP:</b><br /><br />1) <i>Thoughtful and moving.</i><br />“Okay for Now” is a thoughtful and surprisingly moving story about connections between people in a cozy small-town community. In a subtle, “show, don’t tell” manner, Schmidt demonstrates us the need to understand where people are coming from before writing them off as well as the importance of helping each other to overcome struggles.<br /><br />2) <i>Realistic and complex characters.</i><br />Even though there are quite a few characters in this book, they ALL are very realistic and multidimensional, and throughout the book you can clearly see them growing (or most of them, anyways).<br /><br />3) <i>The power of art.</i><br />Each chapter begins with a black-and-white print from John James Audubon’s “Birds of America,” which are skillfully incorporated into the story. In the same “show, don’t tell” manner, Schmidt illustrates the power of art and creativity in dealing with bullying, violence, abuse, war scars and other struggles. Although it’s not a very novel idea, Schmidt conveys it masterfully.<br /><br />4) <i>Engaging.</i><br />“Okay for Now” is narrated from Doug’s point of view and is done so in a fun and an EXTREMELY engaging way. It truly feels like Doug is talking directly to you, asking you questions and even scolding you for not paying attention to what he is saying.<br /><br />5) <i>Uplifting.</i><br />Doug, just like any other teenage boy, occasionally lashes out and misbehaves, but essentially is a good-natured kid, and thus he views the world around him, even the most hostile situations, with a childlike innocence and optimism.<br /><br /><b>COULD BE BETTER:</b><br /><br />1) <i>Ugly cover.</i><br />If possible, get the paperback edition of “Okay for Now,” because the cover of the hardcover is beyond off-putting.<br /><br />2) <i>Unrealistic ending.</i><br />The ending of the book is pleasing, but it seems a little bit too easy, too perfect and thus slightly unrealistic. However, the whole story is so well-crafted that even a lesser ending couldn’t spoil it.<br /><br /><b>VERDICT: 4.5 OUT OF 5</b><br /><br />“Okay for Now” by Gary Schmidt is a thoughtful, uplifting and engaging young adult novel, full of realistic and complex characters. It emphasizes the importance of understanding and connections between people as well as the power of art and creativity in overcoming hardships. It’s one of those books that even an ugly cover or a slightly unrealistic ending cannot spoil.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16099461902513938754" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_207273435" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/207273435" />
    <a title="JE" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3125317-je"><img alt="JE" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1302475011p2/3125317.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/207273435?book_show_action=true">Sep 11, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="JE" class="user" itemprop="url" name="JE" href="/user/show/3125317-je">JE</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3125317-je?shelf=smart-remarks-book-club">smart-remarks-book-club</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3125317-je?shelf=bsw-book-club">bsw-book-club</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer207273435" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16198028602194535329">Doug Swieteck is real. I swear he's real. I'm not lying.<br/><br/>This is the kind of book that when you set it down and look up, you have to blink a few times before you remember where you are. And when reality hits you, you get this sinking sensation in your chest and you feel like crying. Because in reality, there isn't a fourteen-year-old boy named Doug Swieteck who just moved into a dumpy house in a small town in New York. It isn't 1969, the Vietnam War is long over, and space travel has lost its won</span>
  <span id="freeText16198028602194535329" style="display:none">Doug Swieteck is real. I swear he's real. I'm not lying.<br /><br />This is the kind of book that when you set it down and look up, you have to blink a few times before you remember where you are. And when reality hits you, you get this sinking sensation in your chest and you feel like crying. Because in reality, there isn't a fourteen-year-old boy named Doug Swieteck who just moved into a dumpy house in a small town in New York. It isn't 1969, the Vietnam War is long over, and space travel has lost its wonder. But when you're reading this book, I promise you, it all feels real. Doug is real and his brother is a jerk and his principal is a jerk and so is his gym coach and I swear, if I was walking down the street and I passed by Doug Swieteck’s quick-handed, heavy drinkin’, no-good father, I’d punch him in the face, and my only regret would be that my arm has zero muscle mass. When you find out what he did, you'll wanna get in line and do the same. <br /><br />A lot of adults in Doug Swieteck's life have let him down. This book will make you cry, it will break your heart, but it is not a sad book. It is about redemption and the power we have to not only redeem ourselves, but inspire others to do the same through our actions. It'll make you want to be a better person. It'll make you want to try to understand the people who let you down in life. <br /><br />Remember when I said Doug Swieteck isn't real? Well I lied. Doug Swieteck is real. Read this book and you'll see. <br /><br />(Thanks for the recommendation, LaNae!)</span>
  <a data-text-id="16198028602194535329" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cait Poytress" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cait Poytress" href="/user/show/1419944-cait-poytress">Cait Poytress</a>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1419944-cait-poytress?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1419944-cait-poytress?shelf=read-in-2011">read-in-2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1419944-cait-poytress?shelf=ya">ya</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer159020908" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14256028384502714873">I don't know you, Gary D. Schmidt, but I love you. I had to stop reading your latest book while in the doctor's waiting room because it was making me cry. This was literally about 30 seconds after it made me laugh out loud. Some of Doug's one liners were so hilarious I had to read them to my oldest daughter. And you know that noise you sometimes make when you are trying so hard not to cry, that little squeak that escapes from your tightly pursed lips? No? Oh, me either. That totally didn't happe</span>
  <span id="freeText14256028384502714873" style="display:none">I don't know you, Gary D. Schmidt, but I love you. I had to stop reading your latest book while in the doctor's waiting room because it was making me cry. This was literally about 30 seconds after it made me laugh out loud. Some of Doug's one liners were so hilarious I had to read them to my oldest daughter. And you know that noise you sometimes make when you are trying so hard not to cry, that little squeak that escapes from your tightly pursed lips? No? Oh, me either. That totally didn't happen to me when Doug broke down and told Mr. Ferris about his "birthday present". Your emotional roller coaster, Mr. Schmidt, makes for one exhilarating ride.<br /><br />My only complaint is with the final (10? 20?) pages of the book. <br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I am just not satisfied with the way that everyone - especially the biggest mother effers in the book - magically redeemed themselves in the end. I could see it being plausible for the So-Called Gym Teacher, whose horrible behavior could very well be explained by his recent experiences in Vietnam. It is also fitting for Doug's older brothers, who are both struggling so hard against becoming their father. But Principal Peattie? He just all of a sudden becomes this warm, smiling, apologetic benefactor? What the hell? The deplorable way he treated Doug, the things he said to him... and as the principal of the school! And anyone that refers to himself in the third person - and only in the third person - deserves to be punchisized in the face. Not redeemed. BUT. Of all the assholes in this book, Doug's father takes the freaking cake. I just can't believe that this drunken, abusive POS all of a sudden gets a heart and a conscience. A man that treats his family like absolute garbage for years upon years and doesn't seem to give a rat's ass about anyone other than himself and all the ways in which everyone else is out to screw him over. He should be in jail for what he did to Doug! Not once in the first 9/10 of the book is there even the tiniest shred of evidence that he has any good in him, so where the hell did the last 10-20 pages of the book come from? Who is that guy sitting at the dinner table? I just don't get it. My ideal ending would have had Doug's dad either getting a hefty jail sentence for the crimes or maybe dying in a freak accident at the paper mill. I'm not really picky as long as he was out of the picture. Then Doug's mom could've married Mr. Big Bucks Ballard. There's your happy ending. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Ok, so I guess the ending bothered me a lot more than I thought it did. But you know what? I'm still giving your book 5 stars. Because I fell in love with almost every character in this book, especially Doug. Because you managed to make me both sick about the ways in which people are cruel to one another and in awe of the generosity of others. Because you stitched my heart back together just as many times as you broke it, and left me grateful for the experience rather than regretting it. I just ask that if you ever write a sequel, please keep in mind the alternate ending I mentioned above. It's not too late for a relapse, if you catch my drift.<br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_548967819" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/548967819" />
    <a title="Sam Fletcher" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16739663-sam-fletcher"><img alt="Sam Fletcher" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/548967819?book_show_action=true">Feb 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sam Fletcher" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sam Fletcher" href="/user/show/16739663-sam-fletcher">Sam Fletcher</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer548967819" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3189817017731104094">This is honestly one of the most special books I've ever read. on several occasions I was moved to tears. I almost can't even formulate a proper thought. <br/><br/>The writing style is so simple, through the voice of this young boy. Doug personally experiences so much, as well as his family. His story is almost equally tragic as it is beautiful. <br/><br/>The book started off pretty slow, but the pace picked up when the family made the move to Marysville. That's when I was unable to put the book down. That's when I</span>
  <span id="freeText3189817017731104094" style="display:none">This is honestly one of the most special books I've ever read. on several occasions I was moved to tears. I almost can't even formulate a proper thought. <br /><br />The writing style is so simple, through the voice of this young boy. Doug personally experiences so much, as well as his family. His story is almost equally tragic as it is beautiful. <br /><br />The book started off pretty slow, but the pace picked up when the family made the move to Marysville. That's when I was unable to put the book down. That's when I fell in love. Doug grew, and I felt as though I was growing and learning and falling in love with him. All of the relationships were so well-crafted and developed. My heart broke when Lil got sick, but soared when Doug stayed by her side and only loved her more. <br />The only thing I didn't like was Doug's father. He treated the family so poorly, and suddenly had a change of heart? It didn't make much sense. I'm trying to figure out why Schmidt did that.<br /><br />Overall, I loved this book. And I plan on recommending it to anyone who will listen. This story is about so much more then what's on the surface. I feel like all readers connect with it in a different way. Discovering that is the best part.<br /><br />"That smile."</span>
  <a data-text-id="3189817017731104094" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer16655702279943684989">I ate this book up in 2 days, it was that great. As protagonist Doug would say: "Do you know what that feels like!?" I do- "Terrific", as he would also say. The main reason this YA book was so good is because of Doug himself- the 14 year old narrator of the novel. He often repeats the same phrases, making him become a familiar frie</span>
  <span id="freeText16655702279943684989" style="display:none">I ate this book up in 2 days, it was that great. As protagonist Doug would say: "Do you know what that feels like!?" I do- "Terrific", as he would also say. The main reason this YA book was so good is because of Doug himself- the 14 year old narrator of the novel. His VOICE is so distinctly HIS, that readers can easily feel what he does about the various events in his life both good (learning to draw) and bad (his abusive dad). He often repeats the same phrases, making him become a familiar friend whilst reading. This book WOULD have gotten the coveted 5 star rating if not for the ending surrounding the character of Lil- it felt rushed and tacked on, for no real reason. I am sure others will disagree with me on this, but I remain firm that it was not well developed. I won't explain further for fear of ruining anything. But, again, the narration of Doug really shaped this book into a special one. :0)</span>

<div id="review_254051853" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/254051853" />
    <a title="April" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1405910-april"><img alt="April" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574686846p2/1405910.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/254051853?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="April" class="user" itemprop="url" name="April" href="/user/show/1405910-april">April</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1405910-april?shelf=audio">audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1405910-april?shelf=middle-grade">middle-grade</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1405910-april?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1405910-april?shelf=library">library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1405910-april?shelf=highly-recommended">highly-recommended</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer254051853" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3070683512548563370">I’m absolutely in love with Okay For Now by Gary D. Scmidt. Whether it’s the endearing characters -in particular Doug Swieteck who worships Joe Pepitone and is concerned about being a chump, the multiple themes that all built on each other, the late 1960s setting in MY state or Scmidt’s capture of the essence of growing up. Frankly, it’s love. My feelings for Okay For Now, that is.<br/><br/><a href='http://www.goodbooksandgoodwine.com/2012/02/okay-for-now-gary-d-schmidt-audiobook-review.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>Read the rest of my review here</a><br/></span>
  <span id="freeText3070683512548563370" style="display:none">I’m absolutely in love with Okay For Now by Gary D. Scmidt. Whether it’s the endearing characters -in particular Doug Swieteck who worships Joe Pepitone and is concerned about being a chump, the multiple themes that all built on each other, the late 1960s setting in MY state or Scmidt’s capture of the essence of growing up. Frankly, it’s love. My feelings for Okay For Now, that is.<br /><br /><a href="http://www.goodbooksandgoodwine.com/2012/02/okay-for-now-gary-d-schmidt-audiobook-review.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Read the rest of my review here</a><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3070683512548563370" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_477416180" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/477416180" />
    <a title="Ceecee" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10394610-ceecee"><img alt="Ceecee" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1456943309p2/10394610.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/477416180?book_show_action=true">Dec 14, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ceecee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ceecee" href="/user/show/10394610-ceecee">Ceecee</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fans of Louis Sachar, Disney, or The Wonder Years vibe</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Ceecee by: </span>
      Emily May
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10394610-ceecee?shelf=slice-of-life">slice-of-life</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10394610-ceecee?shelf=5-star">5-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10394610-ceecee?shelf=coming-of-age">coming-of-age</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10394610-ceecee?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10394610-ceecee?shelf=i-love-fluff">i-love-fluff</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10394610-ceecee?shelf=lovely-prose">lovely-prose</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10394610-ceecee?shelf=7-stars">7-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10394610-ceecee?shelf=fictional-boyfriends">fictional-boyfriends</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer477416180" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3912477332215468928"><b>I hate Doug Swieteck.</b><br/><br/>I hate what a chump he is, that he's a bundle of insecurities, conflicts, trying to be a tough guy, but really he's soft inside. You can't believe how much I dislike "bad boys", I can't believe Doug could make me hate him so much. Here's this troubled kid, with quite a shitty dad, a troublesome brother, thank God he has a wonderful mother,and this new kid in town, Doug, no one likes him, he's not exactly Holling Hoodhood, but he's a really good kid. A really good kid. And th</span>
  <span id="freeText3912477332215468928" style="display:none"><b>I hate Doug Swieteck.</b><br /><br />I hate what a chump he is, that he's a bundle of insecurities, conflicts, trying to be a tough guy, but really he's soft inside. You can't believe how much I dislike "bad boys", I can't believe Doug could make me hate him so much. Here's this troubled kid, with quite a shitty dad, a troublesome brother, thank God he has a wonderful mother,and this new kid in town, Doug, no one likes him, he's not exactly Holling Hoodhood, but he's a really good kid. A really good kid. And that's why I hate him so much. <b>He makes me care about him so much. Do you know what that feels like?</b> <br /><br />If I haven't become a full fledged fan of Gary D. Schmidt in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/556136.The_Wednesday_Wars" title="The Wednesday Wars by Gary D. Schmidt" rel="nofollow noopener">The Wednesday Wars</a>, I certainly have become one in <b>Okay For Now</b>. You can just tell from the title that there's gonna be heartbreak in this story. <b>*Spoiler alert*</b> Yeah, there is.<br /><br />But, you know what, there's gonna be solace, too. <br /><br />Doug Swieteck is a very endearing kid. I loved his voice. And though I saw elements similar to The Wednesday Wars, it was alright. Disney manages to produce films with the same elements, and yet we flock to their films over and over again. They make one feel good.<br /><br />Like in TWW, Schmidt used forms of art - literature, poetry and paintings from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/155842.Birds_of_America" title="Birds of America by John James Audubon" rel="nofollow noopener">Birds of America</a> in Okay For Now - to mirror the protagonist's conflicts and growth as we read through the story. I have to say I love that style. I've always wanted to write in that style, because it's true: Art mirrors Life.<br /><br />Schmidt knows how to write delightfully flawed characters. And the gem of this book was Doug.<br /><br /><b>*7 stars</b> One of the most delightful heroes I've read. I can't believe I could find another guy I like more than Holling, but I did. Prepare to dislike or utterly hate Doug for it. <br /><br />**P.s. If you haven't read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/556136.The_Wednesday_Wars" title="The Wednesday Wars by Gary D. Schmidt" rel="nofollow noopener">The Wednesday Wars</a>, then what are you waiting for?</span>
  <a data-text-id="3912477332215468928" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_136793948" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/136793948" />
    <a title="Kate" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/151597-kate"><img alt="Kate" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442480463p2/151597.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/136793948?book_show_action=true">Dec 27, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kate" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kate" href="/user/show/151597-kate">Kate</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer136793948" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2316850939875443331">The last book I read over the holiday break was an e-galley of Gary Schmidt's OKAY FOR NOW, a fantastic and (is it possible?) even stronger follow-up to THE WEDNESDAY WARS. It's a companion book, rather than a sequel, since this one is told through the eyes of Holling Hoodhood's classmate, 14-year-old Doug Swieteck as his family moves to a stupid new town where he has no friends and where everyone seems intent on judging him based on the reputation of his scofflaw older brother. The voice in thi</span>
  <span id="freeText2316850939875443331" style="display:none">The last book I read over the holiday break was an e-galley of Gary Schmidt's OKAY FOR NOW, a fantastic and (is it possible?) even stronger follow-up to THE WEDNESDAY WARS. It's a companion book, rather than a sequel, since this one is told through the eyes of Holling Hoodhood's classmate, 14-year-old Doug Swieteck as his family moves to a stupid new town where he has no friends and where everyone seems intent on judging him based on the reputation of his scofflaw older brother. The voice in this book is laugh-out-loud funny, pitch-perfect teen boy, and rings true in all the best ways. I love-love-love the characters that populate these pages, from Doug himself to the stern librarian who begrudingly lets him in every Saturday to ogle the Audobon prints upstairs, to the scary old writer-lady on the delivery route he runs for the local store. (I love that she calls him only "Skinny Delivery Boy" even after they strike up what constitutes an honest-to-goodness friendship.)<br /><br />Like the WEDNESDAY WARS, the humor in this book makes the poignant scenes even more powerful, and there's no shortage of them when Doug's older brother returns from Vietnam seriously hurt and facing all the community attitudes that met returning veterans in that era. OKAY FOR NOW made me laugh and cry, sometimes on the very same page, a sure way for a book to win my heart. Due out in April from Clarion.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2316850939875443331" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3381954641" />
    <a title="Beth Anne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4728103-beth-anne"><img alt="Beth Anne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564681652p2/4728103.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3381954641?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Beth Anne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Beth Anne" href="/user/show/4728103-beth-anne">Beth Anne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4728103-beth-anne?shelf=2020">2020</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3381954641" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4080608124877746019">Timing is everything, especially for me and books. I started Okay for Now back in #middlegrademarch as an audiobook and stopped after less than 10 minutes because it was just too much. I picked up the physical copy from my shelf this week and blew through the whole book in 3 days. (Dare I say it, better than The Wednesday Wars)<br/><br/>Okay for Now, set in 1968, addresses many injustices associated with poverty including poor grades in school, prejudice from teachers, and wrongful accusation of crime. Bu</span>
  <span id="freeText4080608124877746019" style="display:none">Timing is everything, especially for me and books. I started Okay for Now back in #middlegrademarch as an audiobook and stopped after less than 10 minutes because it was just too much. I picked up the physical copy from my shelf this week and blew through the whole book in 3 days. (Dare I say it, better than The Wednesday Wars)<br /><br />Okay for Now, set in 1968, addresses many injustices associated with poverty including poor grades in school, prejudice from teachers, and wrongful accusation of crime. But in the midst of these challenges, Okay for Now weaves together art and friendship and literature as conduits of hope in suffering.<br /><br />I fell hard for the characters in these pages, vivid and full of life. Schmidt peels back layers to show the depth of the brokenness in all of us, and how our lives can be connected in ways that our hurts provide greater empathy, which can lead to redemption. Absolutely pick up the physical copy of this instead of audio - the pictures throughout are frequently referenced in the novel.<br /><br />This is a book for kids (one I can't wait for mine to read), but its truly a book for all ages, and especially an encouragement for adults in any sort of authority on the power of our words and actions in the lives of children.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4080608124877746019" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('WDhiewxntje1vcFE2KCjXEU9RKECYwBshkAjjk8zwFXhN+0PK8rUMBvlmb0Jpsraq2Auk75d5ITM8SNld1U66g==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('hfUdgpOd57h3o9LXq4ocIMmL3fv+jRTHG7wszpULgzI8+pL2tDCFv9n7ii56jHWmJ9a3yUKz8C9RDSwlrW15jQ==')})
  }

Instead of family, there are students -- Eskimo, Indian, White -- who line up on different sides of the cafeteria like there\'s some kind of war going on. And instead of comforting words like tutu and maktak, there\'s English. Speaking I\'nupiaq -- or any native language -- is forbidden. And Father Mullen, whose fury is like a force of nature, is ready to slap down those who disobey. Luke struggles to survive at Sacred Heart. But he\'s not the only one. There\'s smart-aleck Amiq, a daring leader -- if he doesn\'t self destruct; Chickie, blond and freckled, a different kind of outsider; and small quiet Junior, noticing everything and writing it all down. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer17266622298498729957">Gary D. Schmidt is an American children's writer of nonfiction books and young adult novels, including two Newbery Honor books. He lives on a farm in Alto, Michigan,with his wife and six children, where he splits wood, plants gardens, writes, feeds the wild cats that drop by and wishes that sometimes the sea breeze came that far inland. He is a Professor of English at Calvin College.<br/><br/></span>
